                    if (navigator.connection.saveData != true) {

                        preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41586"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="A chromosome conformation capture ordered sequence of the barley genome"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2017 544:7651"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2017-04-26"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2017 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Cereal grasses of the Triticeae tribe have been the major food source in temperate regions since the dawn of agriculture. Their large genomes are characterized by a high content of repetitive elements and large pericentromeric regions that are virtually devoid of meiotic recombination. Here we present a high-quality reference genome assembly for barley (Hordeum vulgare L.). We use chromosome conformation capture mapping to derive the linear order of sequences across the pericentromeric space and to investigate the spatial organization of chromatin in the nucleus at megabase resolution. The composition of genes and repetitive elements differs between distal and proximal regions. Gene family analyses reveal lineage-specific duplications of genes involved in the transport of nutrients to developing seeds and the mobilization of carbohydrates in grains. We demonstrate the importance of the barley reference sequence for breeding by inspecting the genomic partitioning of sequence variation in modern elite germplasm, highlighting regions vulnerable to genetic erosion. The International Barley Genome Sequencing Consortium reports sequencing and assembly of a reference genome for barley, Hordeum vulgare. Triticeae grasses, which include barley, wheat and rye, are widely cultivated plants with particularly complex genomes and evolutionary histories. Sequencing of the barley genome has been particularly challenging owing to its large size and particular genomic features, such as an abundance of repetitive elements. Nils Stein and colleagues of the International Barley Genome Sequencing Consortium report sequencing and assembly of a reference genome for barley (Hordeumvulgare L). They use a combined approach of hierarchical shotgun sequencing of bacterial artificial chromosomes, genome mapping on nanochannel arrays and chromosome-scale scaffolding with Hi-C sequencing. This brings the first comprehensive, completely ordered assembly of the pericentromeric regions of a Triticeae genome. The authors also sequenced and examined genetic diversity in the exomes of 96 European elite barley lines with a spring or winter growth habit, and highlight the utility of this resource for cereal genomics and breeding programs."/>

            <li class="c-article-subject-list__subject"><a href="/subjects/agricultural-genetics" data-track="click" data-track-action="view subject" data-track-label="link" itemprop="about">Agricultural genetics</a></li><li class="c-article-subject-list__subject"><a href="/subjects/dna-sequencing" data-track="click" data-track-action="view subject" data-track-label="link" itemprop="about">DNA sequencing</a></li><li class="c-article-subject-list__subject"><a href="/subjects/genome" data-track="click" data-track-action="view subject" data-track-label="link" itemprop="about">Genome</a></li><li class="c-article-subject-list__subject"><a href="/subjects/natural-variation-in-plants" data-track="click" data-track-action="view subject" data-track-label="link" itemprop="about">Natural variation in plants</a></li><li class="c-article-subject-list__subject"><a href="/subjects/plant-genetics" data-track="click" data-track-action="view subject" data-track-label="link" itemprop="about">Plant genetics</a></li>
        </ul>

            </div>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Cereal grasses of the Triticeae tribe have been the major food source in temperate regions since the dawn of agriculture. Their large genomes are characterized by a high content of repetitive elements and large pericentromeric regions that are virtually devoid of meiotic recombination. Here we present a high-quality reference genome assembly for barley (<i>Hordeum vulgare</i> L.). We use chromosome conformation capture mapping to derive the linear order of sequences across the pericentromeric space and to investigate the spatial organization of chromatin in the nucleus at megabase resolution. The composition of genes and repetitive elements differs between distal and proximal regions. Gene family analyses reveal lineage-specific duplications of genes involved in the transport of nutrients to developing seeds and the mobilization of carbohydrates in grains. We demonstrate the importance of the barley reference sequence for breeding by inspecting the genomic partitioning of sequence variation in modern elite germplasm, highlighting regions vulnerable to genetic erosion.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Barley remains dated to the dawn of agriculture have been found at several archaeological sites<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="van Zeist, W. &amp; Bakker-Heeres, J. A. H. Archaeological studies in the Levant 1. Neolithic sites in the Damascus basin: Aswad, Ghoraifé, Ramad. Palaeohistoria 24, 165–256 (1985)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1852">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Riehl, S., Zeidi, M. &amp; Conard, N. J. Emergence of agriculture in the foothills of the Zagros Mountains of Iran. Science 341, 65–67 (2013)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1855">2</a></sup>. In addition to indications that barley was an important food crop, recent excavations have fuelled speculation that beverages from fermented grains may have motivated early Neolithic hunter–gatherers to erect some of humankind’s oldest monuments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Dietrich, O., Heun, M., Notroff, J., Schmidt, K. &amp; Zarnkow, M. The role of cult and feasting in the emergence of Neolithic communities. New evidence from Göbekli Tepe, south-eastern Turkey. Antiquity 86, 674–695 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1859">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Hayden, B., Canuel, N. &amp; Shanse, J. What was brewing in the Natufian? An archaeological assessment of brewing technology in the Epipaleolithic. J. Archaeol. Method Theory 20, 102–150 (2013)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1862">4</a></sup>. Moreover, brewing beer may also have played a role in the eastward spread of the crop after its initial domestication in the Fertile Crescent<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Wang, J. et al. Revealing a 5,000-y-old beer recipe in China. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 6444–6448 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1866">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Zohary, D ., Hopf, M. &amp; Weiss, E. Domestication of Plants in the Old World: The Origin and Spread of Domesticated Plants in Southwest Asia, Europe, and the Mediterranean Basin (Oxford Univ. Press, 2012)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1869">6</a></sup>.</p><p>Since 2012, both genetic research and crop improvement in barley have benefited from a partly ordered draft sequence assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="International Barley Genome Sequencing Consortium. A physical, genetic and functional sequence assembly of the barley genome. Nature 491, 711–716 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1876">7</a></sup>. This community resource has underpinned gene isolation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Yang, P. et al. PROTEIN DISULFIDE ISOMERASE LIKE 5-1 is a susceptibility factor to plant viruses. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, 2104–2109 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1880">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Pourkheirandish, M. et al. Evolution of the grain dispersal system in barley. Cell 162, 527–539 (2015)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1883">9</a></sup> and population genomic studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Russell, J. et al. Exome sequencing of geographically diverse barley landraces and wild relatives gives insights into environmental adaptation. Nat. Genet. 48, 1024–1030 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1887">10</a></sup>. However, these and other efforts have also revealed limitations of the current draft assembly. The limitations are often direct consequences of two characteristic genomic features: the extreme abundance of repetitive elements, and the severely reduced frequency of meiotic recombination in pericentromeric regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Künzel, G., Korzun, L. &amp; Meister, A. Cytologically integrated physical restriction fragment length polymorphism maps for the barley genome based on translocation breakpoints. Genetics 154, 397–412 (2000)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1891">11</a></sup>. These factors have limited the contiguity of whole-genome assemblies to kilobase-sized sequences originating from low-copy regions of the genome. Thus, a detailed investigation of the composition of the repetitive fraction of the genome—including expanded gene families—and of the distribution of targets of selection and crop improvement in (genetically defined) pericentromeric regions has been beyond reach.</p><p>Here we present a map-based reference sequence of the barley genome including the first comprehensively ordered assembly of the pericentromeric regions of a Triticeae genome. The resource highlights a conspicuous distinction between distal and proximal regions of chromosomes that is reflected by the intranuclear chromatin organization. Moreover, chromosomal compartments are differentiated by an exponential gradient of gene density and recombination rate, striking contrasts in the distribution of retrotransposon families, and distinct patterns of genetic diversity.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="A chromosome-scale assembly of the barley genome"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">A chromosome-scale assembly of the barley genome</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>We adopted a hierarchical approach to generate a high-quality reference genome sequence of the barley cultivar Morex, a US spring six-row malting barley. First, a total of 87,075 bacterial artificial chromosomes (BACs) were sequenced, mainly using Illumina paired-end and mate-pair technology and assembled individually from 4.5 terabases of raw sequence data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Beier, S. et al. Multiplex sequencing of bacterial artificial chromosomes for assembling complex plant genomes. Plant Biotechnol. J. 14, 1511–1522 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1906">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Muñoz-Amatriaín, M. et al. Sequencing of 15 622 gene-bearing BACs clarifies the gene-dense regions of the barley genome. Plant J. 84, 216–227 (2015)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1909">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ounit, R., Wanamaker, S., Close, T. J. &amp; Lonardi, S. CLARK: fast and accurate classification of metagenomic and genomic sequences using discriminative k-mers. BMC Genomics 16, 236 (2015)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1912">14</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 1</a>). In a second step, overlaps between adjacent clones<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Colmsee, C. et al. BARLEX - the Barley Draft Genome Explorer. Mol. Plant 8, 964–966 (2015)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1919">15</a></sup> were detected and validated by physical map information<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Ariyadasa, R. et al. A sequence-ready physical map of barley anchored genetically by two million single-nucleotide polymorphisms. Plant Physiol. 164, 412–423 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1923">16</a></sup>, a genetic linkage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Mascher, M. et al. Anchoring and ordering NGS contig assemblies by population sequencing (POPSEQ). Plant J. 76, 718–727 (2013)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1927">17</a></sup> and a highly contiguous optical map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Lam, E. T. et al. Genome mapping on nanochannel arrays for structural variation analysis and sequence assembly. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 771–776 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1932">18</a></sup> to construct super-scaffolds composed of merged assemblies of individual BACs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Tab1">Table 1</a> and Extended Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Tab1">Table 1</a>). This increased the contiguity as measured by the N50 value (the scaffold size above which 50% of the total length of the sequence was included in the assembly) from 79 kb to 1.9 Mb. Scaffolds were assigned to chromosomes using a population sequencing (POPSEQ) genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Mascher, M. et al. Anchoring and ordering NGS contig assemblies by population sequencing (POPSEQ). Plant J. 76, 718–727 (2013)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1942">17</a></sup>. Finally, we used three-dimensional proximity information obtained by chromosome conformation capture sequencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Kalhor, R., Tjong, H., Jayathilaka, N., Alber, F. &amp; Chen, L. Genome architectures revealed by tethered chromosome conformation capture and population-based modeling. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 90–98 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1946">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Lieberman-Aiden, E. et al. Comprehensive mapping of long-range interactions reveals folding principles of the human genome. Science 326, 289–293 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1949">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119–1125 (2013)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1952">21</a></sup> (Hi-C) to order and orient BAC-based super-scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 2</a> and ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Beier, S. et al. Construction of a map-based reference genome sequence for barley, Hordeum vulgare L. Sci. Data 4, 170044 (2017)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1959">22</a>). The final chromosome-scale assembly of the barley genome consists of 6,347 ordered super-scaffolds composed of merged assemblies of individual BACs, representing 4.79 Gb (~ 95%) of the genomic sequence content, of which 4.54 Gb have been assigned to precise chromosomal location in the Hi-C map (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Tab1">Table 1</a>). Mapping of transcriptome data and reference protein sequences from other plant species to the assembly identified 83,105 putative gene loci including protein-coding genes, non-coding RNAs, pseudogenes and transcribed transposons (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig6">Extended Data Fig. 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Tab2">Extended Data Table 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 3</a>). These loci were filtered further and divided into 39,734 high-confidence genes (with four different sub-categories) and 41,949 low-confidence genes on the basis of sequence homology to related species (Methods and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 3.4</a>). Moreover, we predicted 19,908 long non-coding RNAs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 3.7</a>) and 792 microRNA precursor loci (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 3.8</a>). The high co-linearity between the Hi-C-based pseudomolecules and linkage and cytogenetic maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Beier, S. et al. Construction of a map-based reference genome sequence for barley, Hordeum vulgare L. Sci. Data 4, 170044 (2017)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1988">22</a></sup> as well as the conserved order of syntenic genes in pericentromeric regions compared with model grass <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig7">Extended Data Fig. 2a</a>) corroborated the quality of the assembly. Extrapolating from a set of conserved eukaryotic core genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d14867e1999">23</a></sup>, we estimate that the predicted gene models represent 98% of the cultivar Morex barley gene complement (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig7">Extended Data Fig. 2b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Assembly and annotation statistics</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nature22043/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Characteristics of genomic compartments in barley chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Characteristics of genomic compartments in barley chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature22043/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22043/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature22043_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22043/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature22043_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1402" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, The distribution of genomic features in 4 Mb windows is plotted along chromosome 1H. Analogous panels for the other chromosomes are found in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig10">Extended Data Fig. 5a</a>. The left column in the legend refers to the background shading in the top panel; the right column indicates the colour code for lines in both panels. CDS, predicted coding sequences; cM, centimorgans. <b>b</b>, Enrichment of Gene Ontology (GO) terms in genomic compartments. Coloured rectangles indicate enrichment factors ranging from −2 (dark blue) to 2 (dark red). Numbers inside the rectangles indicate −log<sub>10</sub>-transformed <i>P</i> values.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM35">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature22043/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec3" data-title="Organization of chromatin"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec3">Organization of chromatin</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec3-content"><p>Barley has served as a model for traditional cytogenetics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Künzel, G., Korzun, L. &amp; Meister, A. Cytologically integrated physical restriction fragment length polymorphism maps for the barley genome based on translocation breakpoints. Genetics 154, 397–412 (2000)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2235">11</a></sup>; but relating chromosomal features to unique sequences has been challenging, requiring the cloning of repeat-free probes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Fuchs, J., Houben, A., Brandes, A. &amp; Schubert, I. Chromosome ‘painting’ in plants - a feasible technique? Chromosoma 104, 315–320 (1996)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2239">24</a></sup>. The reference sequence allowed us to employ the Hi-C data to interrogate the three-dimensional organization of chromatin in the nucleus. As in other eukaryotes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Lieberman-Aiden, E. et al. Comprehensive mapping of long-range interactions reveals folding principles of the human genome. Science 326, 289–293 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2243">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Grob, S., Schmid, M. W. &amp; Grossniklaus, U. Hi-C analysis in Arabidopsis identifies the KNOT, a structure with similarities to the flamenco locus of Drosophila. Mol. Cell 55, 678–693 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2246">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Duan, Z. et al. A three-dimensional model of the yeast genome. Nature 465, 363–367 (2010)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2249">26</a></sup>, the spatial proximity of genomic loci as measured by Hi-C link frequency is highly dependent on their distance in the linear genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>). However, we observed an elevated link frequency at distances above 200 Mb and a pronounced anti-diagonal pattern in the intrachromosomal Hi-C contact matrices (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 3a</a>), indicating an increased adjacency of regions on different chromosome arms. We interpret this pattern as reflective of the so-called Rabl configuration<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Tiang, C. L., He, Y. &amp; Pawlowski, W. P. Chromosome organization and dynamics during interphase, mitosis, and meiosis in plants. Plant Physiol. 158, 26–34 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2263">27</a></sup> of interphase nuclei, where individual chromosomes fold back to juxtapose the long and short arms, with centromeres and telomeres of all chromosomes clustering at opposite poles of the nucleus (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Fig. 2.2</a>). Fluorescence <i>in situ</i> hybridization (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig2">Fig. 2d</a>) supported this hypothesis. Principal component analysis of the intrachromosomal proximity matrix showed that the first three principal components cumulatively explained ~70% of the variation and differentiated (1) distal from proximal regions, (2) interstitial from both distal and proximal regions and (3) the long arms from the short arms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig2">Fig. 2f</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig9">Extended Data Fig. 4a</a>). A linear model taking into account the genomic distance between two loci, as well as their relative distance from the centromere, accounted for 79% of the variation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig9">Extended Data Fig. 4b</a>) in the intrachromosomal proximity matrix at 1 Mb resolution.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Chromosome conformation capture analysis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Chromosome conformation capture analysis.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature22043/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22043/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature22043_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22043/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature22043_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="428" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Distance-dependent decay of contact probability. <b>b</b>, Intrachromosomal contact matrix. The intensity of pixels represents the normalized count of Hi-C links between 1 Mb windows on chromosome 1H on a logarithmic scale. <b>c</b>, Schematic model of the Rabl configuration of interphase chromosomes. Centromeres and telomeres are presented by red and green circles, respectively. <b>d</b>, Leaf interphase nucleus of barley. Chromatin was stained blue with 4′,6-diamidino-2-phenylindole (DAPI). Fluorescence <i>in situ</i> hybridization was performed with probes specific for centromeres (red) and telomeres (green). Scale bar, 5 μm. <b>e</b>, Interchromosomal contact matrix. The intensity of pixels represents the normalized count of Hi-C links between 1 Mb windows on chromosomes 1H (<i>x</i> axis) and 2H (<i>y</i> axis) on a logarithmic scale. A principal component analysis of the normalized contact matrix at 1 Mb resolution of chromosome 1H was conducted. <b>f</b>, The first and second eigenvectors are plotted against each other. Each point represents a 1 Mb window. Closer proximity to the centromere is indicated by a darker colour. Windows from the short and long arms are coloured blue and red, respectively.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM36">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature22043/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Contacts between loci on different chromosomes followed a similar pattern (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig2">Fig. 2e</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 3b</a>): a prominent cross pattern supporting a juxtaposition of long and short arms. In contrast to intrachromosomal matrices, contact probabilities between loci on, for instance, the short arm of one chromosome are equal for loci on both the short and the long arm on another chromosome having the same relative distance to the centromeres: that is, facing each other in the interphase nucleus. We also observed a higher contact frequency between telomere-near regions, as has been observed in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Grob, S., Schmid, M. W. &amp; Grossniklaus, U. Hi-C analysis in Arabidopsis identifies the KNOT, a structure with similarities to the flamenco locus of Drosophila. Mol. Cell 55, 678–693 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2353">25</a></sup>.</p><p>To test whether pairs of homologous chromosomes are positioned closer to each other than to non-homologues, we performed diploid Hi-C<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Rao, S. S. et al. A 3D map of the human genome at kilobase resolution reveals principles of chromatin looping. Cell 159, 1665–1680 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2360">28</a></sup> on leaf tissue from F<sub>1</sub> hybrids between the cultivars Morex and Barke, and assigned the resultant Hi-C links to the haplotypes of both inbred parents by mapping reads to a diploid reference. We did not observe any preferential interaction between homologues. Rather, contacts between the maternal and paternal copies of the same chromosome occurred as frequently as between non-homologues (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig9">Extended Data Fig. 4c</a>).</p><p>We conclude that the frequency with which loci juxtapose in three-dimensional space is predominantly determined by their position in the linear genome. This is in sharp contrast to the organization of chromatin in human nuclei where two compartments corresponding to open and closed chromatin domains are evident at megabase resolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Lieberman-Aiden, E. et al. Comprehensive mapping of long-range interactions reveals folding principles of the human genome. Science 326, 289–293 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2372">20</a></sup>, but is consistent with cytogenetic mapping of histone marks associated with heterochromatin in large, repeat-rich genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Houben, A. et al. Methylation of histone H3 in euchromatin of plant chromosomes depends on basic nuclear DNA content. Plant J. 33, 967–973 (2003)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2376">29</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec4" data-title="The genomic context of repetitive elements"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec4-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec4">The genomic context of repetitive elements</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec4-content"><p>Large plant genomes consist mainly of highly similar copies of repetitive elements such as long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons and DNA transposons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Flavell, R. B., Bennett, M. D., Smith, J. B. &amp; Smith, D. B. Genome size and the proportion of repeated nucleotide sequence DNA in plants. Biochem. Genet. 12, 257–269 (1974)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2388">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="SanMiguel, P. et al. Nested retrotransposons in the intergenic regions of the maize genome. Science 274, 765–768 (1996)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2391">31</a></sup>. Our hierarchical sequencing strategy reduced the algorithmic complexity of assembling a highly repetitive genome from short reads. Instead of resolving complex repeat structures on the whole-genome level, we reconstructed the sequences of 100–150 kb BACs. This allowed us to disentangle nearly identical copies of highly abundant repetitive elements, as evidenced by the good representation of both mathematically defined repeats and retrotransposon families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig7">Extended Data Fig. 2c, d</a>). Homology-guided repeat annotation with a Triticeae-specific repeat library<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wicker, T., Matthews, D. E. &amp; Keller, B. TREP: a database for Triticeae repetitive elements. Trends Plant Sci. 7, 561–562 (2002)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2398">32</a></sup> identified 3.7 Gb (80.8%) of the assembled sequence as derived from transposable elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Tab1">Table 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Tab3">Extended Data Table 3</a>), most of which were present as truncated and degenerated copies, with only 10% of mobile elements intact and potentially active.</p><p>Median 20-mer frequencies were used to partition the seven barley chromosomes into three zones (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig10">Extended Data Fig. 5a</a>), reminiscent of the three compartments of wheat chromosome 3B<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Choulet, F. et al. Structural and functional partitioning of bread wheat chromosome 3B. Science 345, 1249721 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2421">33</a></sup>. The distal zone 1 was characterized by an enrichment of low-copy regions, a high gene content and frequent meiotic recombination. Zone 2, occupying the interstitial regions of chromosomes, had the highest 20-mer frequencies and intermediate gene density. Surprisingly, the abundance of repetitive 20-mers decreased in the proximal zone 3, where older mobile elements with diverged, and thus unique, sequences predominated (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). The three zones also differed in the composition of the gene space (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Tab2">Extended Data Table 2b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 3</a>). For example, genes involved in defence response and reproductive processes were preferentially found in distal regions, while proximal regions contained more genes related to housekeeping processes, such as photosynthesis and respiration, compared with other parts of the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>).</p><p>Transposable element groups exhibited pronounced variation in their insertion site preferences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig10">Extended Data Fig. 5b</a>). On a global scale, most miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements and long interspersed elements were found in gene-rich distal regions, as has been reported in other grass species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Bureau, T. E. &amp; Wessler, S. R. Stowaway: a new family of inverted repeat elements associated with the genes of both monocotyledonous and dicotyledonous plants. Plant Cell 6, 907–916 (1994)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2447">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Bureau, T. E. &amp; Wessler, S. R. Mobile inverted-repeat elements of the Tourist family are associated with the genes of many cereal grasses. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 91, 1411–1415 (1994)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2450">35</a></sup>. By contrast, zone 3 was populated by <i>Gypsy</i> retrotransposons, while <i>Copia</i> elements favoured zones 1 and 2. These differences in the relative abundance of retrotransposon families were reflected by distinct distributions of functional domains. For example, sequences encoding the chromodomain (PF00385) are concentrated in the vicinity of the centromere and may be involved in the target specificity through incorporation in the integrase of <i>Gypsy</i> elements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Malik, H. S. &amp; Eickbush, T. H. Modular evolution of the integrase domain in the Ty3/Gypsy class of LTR retrotransposons. J. Virol. 73, 5186–5190 (1999)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2464">36</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig10">Extended Data Fig. 5b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="The genomic context of repetitive elements."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: The genomic context of repetitive elements.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature22043/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22043/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature22043_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22043/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature22043_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="629" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Abundance of key genomic features, different transposon superfamilies and common Pfam domains across chromosome 1H. Analogous panels for the other chromosomes are found in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig10">Extended Data Fig. 5b</a>. The colour scale of the heatmaps ranges from blue (0) to yellow (maximum across all chromosomes per track). Minimum and maximum values are indicated to the right of each track. MITEs, miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements; LINEs, long interspersed elements; fl, full-length; PR, protease; CH, chromodomain; RT, reverse transcriptase; NBS, NB-ARC; Pkin, protein kinase. <b>b</b>, Transposable elements up- and downstream of genes. Coding sequences of high-confidence genes were used as anchor points. Transposable element composition was determined 10 kb up- and downstream of each gene. The <i>x</i> axis indicates the position relative to the gene, while the <i>y</i> axis indicates how many genes had a transposable element of the respective superfamily at the respective position in their upstream/downstream region.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM37">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature22043/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>At a local scale, different types of elements also occupy different niches in the proximity of genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). <i>Mariner</i> transposons preferably reside within 1 kb up- or downstream of the coding regions of genes, while <i>Harbinger</i> and long interspersed elements are found further away. The observed distribution of different types of transposable elements around genes may reflect selective pressures, allowing only the smallest elements, namely <i>Mariners</i>, to be tolerated closest to genes. Intriguingly, <i>Helitrons</i> as well as elements of the <i>Harbinger</i> superfamily have a clear preference for promoter regions, while long interspersed elements have a preference for downstream regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). At greater distances from genes, large elements such as LTR retrotransposons and CACTA elements dominate.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec5" data-title="Expansion of gene families"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec5-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec5">Expansion of gene families</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec5-content"><p>The barley reference sequence enabled us to disentangle complex gene duplications that may shed light on gene family expansion specific to barley or the Triticeae. A total of 29,944 genes belonged to families with multiple members (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 4.1</a>). Gene families expanded in barley were tested for over-representation of Gene Ontology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Harris, M. A. et al. The Gene Ontology (GO) database and informatics resource. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, D258–D261 (2004)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2553">37</a></sup> terms compared with sorghum, rice, <i>Brachypodium</i> and <i>Arabidopsis</i>. Among the most significant results were terms related to defence response and disease resistance (NBS-LRR and thionin genes), as well as thioredoxin genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 4.1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Expansion of agronomically important gene families."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Expansion of agronomically important gene families.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature22043/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22043/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature22043_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22043/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature22043_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="442" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, OrthoMCL clustering of the barley high-confidence gene complement with <i>B. distachyon</i>, rice, sorghum and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> genes. Numbers in the sections of the Venn diagram correspond to numbers of clusters (gene groups). The first number below the species name denotes the total number of proteins that were included into the OrthoMCL analysis for each species. The second number indicates the number of genes in clusters for a species. <b>b</b>, Phylogenetic tree of 68 full-length α-amylase protein sequences derived from amy genes identified in the genomes of barley, hexaploid wheat, <i>B. distachyon</i>, rice, sorghum and maize. Each wheat subgenome was considered separately to facilitate the comparison of gene copy numbers and duplication events across species. Note that for the amy4 subfamily, two to three genes per genome were identified in all genomes. These genes are located on distinct chromosomes and hence most probably did not originate from tandem gene duplications. While most species further contain only a single amy3 gene copy per genome, moderate copy number extension was observed in sorghum and rice where a potential tandem gene duplication resulted in two amy3 gene copies. Three genes of the amy2 subfamily were identified on chromosome 7H in barley and on chromosomes 7A, 7B, 7D in wheat. No similar copy number extension was observed in <i>B. distachyon</i>, <i>Sorghum bicolor</i> or <i>Oryza sativa</i>. In maize, two amy2 genes were identified. The amy1 subfamily shows the highest level of copy number extension. Tandem duplications are present in sorghum and rice. Two to three full-length genes were identified per genome in hexaploid wheat on group 6 chromosomes and five full-length amy1 genes on chromosome 6H and unanchored scaffolds in barley. Notably four of these barley genes share 99.8–100% sequence identity on protein and nucleotide level, indicating very recent duplication events. <i>T. aestivum</i>, <i>Triticum aestivum</i>; <i>Z. mays</i>, <i>Zea mays</i>. <b>c</b>, Expression of the SWEET11 gene subfamily in the developing barley grains. Left, expression profiles of <i>SWEET11a</i> and <i>SWEET11b</i> as determined by quantitative real-time PCR (qPCR) on total RNA isolated from micro-dissected developing grains. Right, localization of <i>SWEET11a</i> and <i>SWEET11b</i> expression in cross-section of immature seeds by RNA <i>in situ</i> hybridization. Hybridizations with sense probes are shown as negative controls in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig12">Extended Data Fig. 7a</a>. Scale bars, 100 μm.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM38">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature22043/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In the following, we focused on a detailed analysis of gene families having particular importance for malting quality. Germinating barley grains possess high diastatic power: that is, the combined ability of a complex of enzymes to mobilize fermentable sugars from starch. Key diastatic enzymes include α-amylases. The genome of barley cultivar Morex contains 12 α-amylase (amy) family sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 4.2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Tab4">Extended Data Table 4a</a>), which can be classified into four subfamilies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Huang, N., Sutliff, T. D., Litts, J. C. &amp; Rodriguez, R. L. Classification and characterization of the rice α-amylase multigene family. Plant Mol. Biol. 14, 655–668 (1990)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2661">38</a></sup>. Gene duplication events have occurred in the subfamilies amy1 and amy2 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>), located on chromosomes 6H and 7H, respectively. The existence of these duplications had been speculated earlier, but could not be analysed further because of high sequence similarity between the copies. The reference assembly contained five full-length amy1 subfamily genes, four of which, here designated as <i>amy1_1a–d</i>, shared &gt;99.8% identity at the nucleotide level including introns. Locus-specific PCR confirmed earlier suggestions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Muthukrishnan, S., Gill, B. S., Swegle, M. &amp; Chandra, G. R. Structural genes for α-amylases are located on barley chromosomes 1 and 6. J. Biol. Chem. 259, 13637–13639 (1984)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2672">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Khursheed, B. &amp; Rogers, J. C. Barley α-amylase genes. Quantitative comparison of steady-state mRNA levels from individual members of the two different families expressed in aleurone cells. J. Biol. Chem. 263, 18953–18960 (1988)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2675">40</a></sup> of multiple, highly similar <i>amy1_1</i> genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig11">Extended Data Fig. 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 4.2</a>). Given the relevance of α-amylase activity to the brewing process, the high variability of the <i>amy1_1</i> multiple gene locus (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig11">Extended Data Fig. 6</a>) observed in landraces and elite lines, including modern malting cultivars, is remarkable.</p><p>The accumulation of fermentable carbohydrates in the grain depends on the transfer of sugars from maternal tissue into the developing seeds. In contrast to the two routes of nutrient transfer in rice seeds—the nucellar projection and nucellar epidermis—delivery of assimilates into barley grains occurs predominantly via the nucellar projection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Melkus, G. et al. Dynamic 13C/1H NMR imaging uncovers sugar allocation in the living seed. Plant Biotechnol. J. 9, 1022–1037 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2697">41</a></sup> and requires active transporters. The family of SUGARS WILL EVENTUALLY BE EXPORTED TRANSPORTER (SWEET) transmembrane proteins mediating sugar efflux<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Chen, L. Q. et al. Sucrose efflux mediated by SWEET proteins as a key step for phloem transport. Science 335, 207–211 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2701">42</a></sup> consists of 23 members in barley (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Tab4">Extended Data Table 4b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 4.3</a>). There is a small extension of the sugar-transporting SWEET11, SWEET13, SWEET14 and SWEET15 subfamilies, with two or more genes for each subgroup compared with only a single orthologue in rice and <i>Arabidopsis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Tab4">Extended Data Table 4b</a>). Duplication of SWEET11 was most likely followed by neofunctionalization as evidenced by divergent expression patterns. Both <i>SWEET11a</i> and <i>SWEET11b</i> were highly expressed in maternal seed tissue, but differed in the distribution of expression domains (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig12">Extended Data Fig. 7</a>). Genes encoding a family of vacuolar processing enzymes, which are essential for programmed cell death in maternal tissue<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Tran, V., Weier, D., Radchuk, R., Thiel, J. &amp; Radchuk, V. Caspase-like activities accompany programmed cell death events in developing barley grains. PLoS ONE 9, e109426 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2730">43</a></sup> and starch accumulation in the grain (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 4.3</a> and V.R., unpublished observations) showed a similar expansion in barley (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Tab4">Extended Data Table 4c</a>), pointing to the central role of the nucellar projection for grain filling in the Triticeae.</p><p>These examples of genes involved in sugar transport and metabolism illustrate that the high-quality reference genome sequence can serve as a springboard for the in-depth analysis of the evolutionary history of gene duplications, their relation to morphological and physiological innovations, and their impact on crop performance.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec6" data-title="Molecular diversity and haplotype analysis"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Molecular diversity and haplotype analysis</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><p>To explore how the new barley genome assembly could be exploited for genetics and breeding, we generated exome sequence data from 96 European elite barley lines, half with a spring growth habit, half with a winter one (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Table 5.1</a>). We investigated the extent and partitioning of molecular variation within and between these groups using 71,285 single-nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs). Plotting diversity values in 100 SNP windows both in linear order (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a>) and according to physical distance (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig5">Fig. 5b</a>) revealed marked contrasts in the levels and distribution of diversity both within and between gene pools. In spring types, extensive regions on chromosomes 1H, 2H and 7H were virtually devoid of diversity, as was a large region on 5H in the winter gene pool. For these chromosomes, this results in a single gene-pool-specific haplotype across the extensive pericentromeric regions. Chromosomes 3H, 4H and 6H maintain higher diversity across these regions owing to the presence of multiple similarly extensive haplotypes. This is even more evident when diversity is plotted on a physical scale (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig5">Fig. 5b</a>). We presume that the lack of observed variation in elite germplasm is a signature of intense selection during breeding for different end-use sectors (principally malting versus feed barley), and the virtual absence of allelic re-assortment during meiosis owing to restricted recombination in the pericentromeric regions.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Distribution of genetic diversity across the barley genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Distribution of genetic diversity across the barley genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature22043/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22043/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature22043_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22043/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature22043_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="617" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Ninety-six elite barley cultivars, including 48 from the winter gene pool (blue line) and 48 from the spring gene pool (red line), were used. Diversity (unbiased heterozygosity, <i>y</i> axis) is plotted as the rolling average of 100 adjacent SNPs along each chromosome. For improved visualization, all chromosomes have been normalized to a standard length. <b>a</b>, Patterns of diversity on chromosomes 1H–7H (top to bottom). The distance between each SNP has been normalized (that is, this does not show genetic distance). The number of SNPs included on each chromosome is given at the bottom right of each plot. <b>b</b>, The same diversity values normalized according to physical distance. Extensive peri-centromeric regions of very low diversity in the spring gene pool are highlighted in green and low diversity in the winter gene pool in purple. Regions with similar levels of diversity in both gene pools are highlighted in orange. Coloured dots show the position of eight loci previously identified as being differentiated between the winter and spring gene pools.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM39">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature22043/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Crosses between spring and winter barleys are rarely performed as they are considered to disrupt the gene-pool-specific gene complexes required for general performance (such as phenological adaptations) and end-use quality. Contrasting local patterns of diversity outside the pericentromeric regions therefore also most likely reflect the outcome of selection within alternative gene pools. We explored this further by comparing diversity in eight characterized genes whose variant alleles are important for conditioning barley’s seasonal growth habit (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 5</a>). Of the eight genes, <i>HvCEN</i> is uniquely ‘locked’ in the pericentromeric region of chromosome 2H where alternative alleles at a single SNP confer both differences in days-to-heading<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Comadran, J. et al. Natural variation in a homolog of Antirrhinum CENTRORADIALIS contributed to spring growth habit and environmental adaptation in cultivated barley. Nat. Genet. 44, 1388–1392 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2809">44</a></sup> and strong latitudinal differentiation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Russell, J. et al. Exome sequencing of geographically diverse barley landraces and wild relatives gives insights into environmental adaptation. Nat. Genet. 48, 1024–1030 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2813">10</a></sup>. The extensive pericentromeric haplotype in spring barleys (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig5">Fig. 5</a>) may stem from selection for this single <i>HvCEN</i> SNP. While strong selection for other favourable alleles locked in the same region in spring barley cannot be ruled out, the virtual absence of recombination severely restricts exploitation of diversity across the entire region. Despite our focus here on life-history traits, strong selection for other traits mapping to pericentromeric regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Schmalenbach, I., Léon, J. &amp; Pillen, K. Identification and verification of QTLs for agronomic traits using wild barley introgression lines. Theor. Appl. Genet. 118, 483–497 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2824">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Han, F. et al. Dissection of a malting quality QTL region on chromosome 1 (7H) of barley. Mol. Breed. 14, 339–347 (2004)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2827">46</a></sup>, including good malting quality in the spring gene pool on chromosomes 1H and 7H, would probably also reduce diversity in these regions. Interestingly, we are unaware of any phenotypic trait in the winter gene pool that would result in strong selection for a single pericentromeric haplotype on chromosome 5H.</p><p>We next explored patterns of linkage disequilibrium across the entire genome. As expected for two highly inbred and elite crop gene pools, we observed extensive linkage disequilibrium on all chromosomes in both spring and winter barleys (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig13">Extended Data Fig. 8</a>). The number of discrete haplotype blocks in this germplasm set varied from 86 to 161 per chromosome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig13">Extended Data Fig. 8</a>). Surprisingly, the two-row spring gene pool, generally considered to be narrowest owing to intense selection for malting quality, exhibited a greater number of haplotype blocks than the winter lines for most chromosomes.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>To assemble a highly contiguous reference genome sequence for barley, we combined hierarchical shotgun sequencing, a strategy previously used for assembling large and complex plant genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Choulet, F. et al. Structural and functional partitioning of bread wheat chromosome 3B. Science 345, 1249721 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2848">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2851">47</a></sup>, with novel technologies such as optical mapping<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Lam, E. T. et al. Genome mapping on nanochannel arrays for structural variation analysis and sequence assembly. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 771–776 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2855">18</a></sup> and chromosome-scale scaffolding with Hi-C<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119–1125 (2013)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2859">21</a></sup>. The latter technology was key to resolving the linear order of sequence scaffolds in pericentromeric regions. We anticipate the adoption of Hi-C-based genome mapping in other Triticeae species, such as bread and durum wheat and their wild relatives. Now that the quality of whole-genome shotgun assemblies is on a par with map-based assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Zimin, A. V. et al. Hybrid assembly of the large and highly repetitive genome of Aegilops tauschii, a progenitor of bread wheat, with the mega-reads algorithm. Preprint at &#xA;                    http://biorxiv.org/content/early/2016/07/26/066100&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2016)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2863">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Pendleton, M. et al. Assembly and diploid architecture of an individual human genome via single-molecule technologies. Nat. Methods 12, 780–786 (2015)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2866">49</a></sup>, we believe that the barley genome project will be one of the last such efforts to follow the laborious BAC-by-BAC approach.</p><p>The barley reference genome sequence constitutes an important community resource for cereal genetics and genomics. It will facilitate positional cloning, provide a better contextualization of population genomic datasets and enable comparative genomic analysis with other Triticeae in non-recombining regions that have been inaccessible to analysis of gene collinearity until now. The exciting methodological advances in sequence assembly and genome mapping have enabled even large and repeat-rich genomes to be unlocked<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Zimin, A. V. et al. Hybrid assembly of the large and highly repetitive genome of Aegilops tauschii, a progenitor of bread wheat, with the mega-reads algorithm. Preprint at &#xA;                    http://biorxiv.org/content/early/2016/07/26/066100&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2016)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2873">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Hirsch, C. et al. Draft assembly of elite inbred line PH207 provides insights into genomic and transcriptome diversity in maize. Plant Cell 28, 2700–2714 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2876">50</a></sup> and hold the promise of constructing reference-quality genome sequences, not only for a single cultivar, but also for representatives of major germplasm groups.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>No statistical methods were used to predetermine sample size. The experiments were not randomized. The investigators were not blinded to allocation during experiments and outcome assessment.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Sequencing and assembly of individual BAC clones</h3><p>Barley genome sequencing relied exclusively on shotgun sequencing of 88,731 BAC clones using high-throughput next-generation sequencing-by-synthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Beier, S. et al. Construction of a map-based reference genome sequence for barley, Hordeum vulgare L. Sci. Data 4, 170044 (2017)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2895">22</a></sup>. This comprised 15,661 so-called gene-bearing BAC clones, preselected mainly by overgo-probe hybridization for the presence of transcribed genes and fingerprinted for definition of a minimum tiling path of the barley gene space.
