These gene-space minimum tiling path BAC clones were sequenced as combinatorial pools by Illumina short-read technology and, after quality trimming of de-convoluted reads, were assembled using Velvet version 1.2.09 as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Muñoz-Amatriaín, M. et al. Sequencing of 15 622 gene-bearing BACs clarifies the gene-dense regions of the barley genome. Plant J. 84, 216–227 (2015)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2899">13</a></sup>. The remaining 73,070 BACs were selected from a minimum tiling path representing the physical map of the barley genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Ariyadasa, R. et al. A sequence-ready physical map of barley anchored genetically by two million single-nucleotide polymorphisms. Plant Physiol. 164, 412–423 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2903">16</a></sup>. Minimum tiling path BAC clones assigned to different barley chromosomes were sequenced at one of four sequencing centres, relying on highly multiplexed paired-end and mate-pair sequencing libraries using either the Roche 454 Titanium or the Illumina MiSeq, HiSeq2000 and HiSeq2500 platforms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 1</a> and ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Chevreux, B., Wetter, T. &amp; Suhai, S. Genome sequence assembly using trace signals and additional sequence information. In Computer Science and Biology: Proc. 99th German Conference on Bioinformatics (eds Hofestädt, R. et al. 45–56 (GCB, 1999)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2909">51</a>). In brief, sequencing reads were de-convoluted on the basis of the used BAC-specific barcode sequence tags and assembled with sequencing centre-specific assembly pipelines. BAC clones sequenced on the Roche 454 Titanium platform were assembled with MIRA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Chevreux, B., Wetter, T. &amp; Suhai, S. Genome sequence assembly using trace signals and additional sequence information. In Computer Science and Biology: Proc. 99th German Conference on Bioinformatics (eds Hofestädt, R. et al. 45–56 (GCB, 1999)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2914">51</a></sup> according to previously described procedures<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Steuernagel, B. et al. De novo 454 sequencing of barcoded BAC pools for comprehensive gene survey and genome analysis in the complex genome of barley. BMC Genomics 10, 547 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2918">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Taudien, S. et al. Sequencing of BAC pools by different next generation sequencing platforms and strategies. BMC Res. Notes 4, 411 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2921">53</a></sup>. Illumina HiSeq2000 paired-end sequencing data (2 × 100 nucleotides) of BAC clones were assembled either with CLC Assembly Cell version 4.0.6 beta (<a href="http://www.clcbio.com/products/clc-assembly-cell/">http://www.clcbio.com/products/clc-assembly-cell/</a>) set to default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Beier, S. et al. Multiplex sequencing of bacterial artificial chromosomes for assembling complex plant genomes. Plant Biotechnol. J. 14, 1511–1522 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2932">12</a></sup>, SOAPdenovo version 2.01 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2935">54</a>) or the ABySS assembler (version 1.5.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Simpson, J. T. et al. ABySS: a parallel assembler for short read sequence data. Genome Res. 19, 1117–1123 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2939">55</a></sup>. Sequence contigs of the <i>de novo</i> BAC assemblies larger than 500 base pairs (bp) were scaffolded using mate-pair sequencing information either generated from BAC DNA-derived 8 kbp insert mate-pair sequencing libraries or from 2 kbp, 5 kbp or 10 kbp genomic DNA-derived mate-pair libraries. This was achieved by either using BWA mem version 0.7.4 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows–Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2946">56</a>) with default parameters for read mapping, followed by scaffolding individual BACs using SSPACE version 3.0 Standard<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V., Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578–579 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2950">57</a></sup>, or with SOAPaligner/soap2 version 2.21 and using SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2954">54</a></sup> scaffolder version 2.01.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Genome-wide three-dimensional chromatin conformation capture sequencing</h3><p>To generate physical scaffolding information for the BAC sequence based genome assembly, as proposed in ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119–1125 (2013)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2965">21</a>, Hi-C and tethered conformation capture (TCC) sequencing data were generated from 7-day-old leaf tissue of greenhouse-grown barley plantlets by adapting previously published procedures (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 2</a>). In brief, for Hi-C, freshly harvested leaves were cut into 2 cm pieces and vacuum infiltrated in nuclei isolation buffer supplemented with 2% formaldehyde. Crosslinking was stopped by adding glycine and additional vacuum infiltration. Fixed tissue was frozen in liquid nitrogen and ground to powder before re-suspending in nuclei isolation buffer to obtain a suspension of nuclei. About 10<sup>7</sup> purified nuclei were digested with 400 units of HindIII as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Belton, J. M. et al. Hi-C: a comprehensive technique to capture the conformation of genomes. Methods 58, 268–276 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2974">58</a></sup>. Digested chromatin was marked by incubating with biotin-14-dCTP and Klenow enzyme using a fill-in reaction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Lieberman-Aiden, E. et al. Comprehensive mapping of long-range interactions reveals folding principles of the human genome. Science 326, 289–293 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2978">20</a></sup> resulting in blunt-ended repaired DNA strands. Biotin-14-dCTP from non-ligated DNA ends was removed owing to the exonuclease activity of T4 DNA polymerase, followed by phenol–chloroform extraction and washing of the precipitated DNA as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Lieberman-Aiden, E. et al. Comprehensive mapping of long-range interactions reveals folding principles of the human genome. Science 326, 289–293 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2983">20</a></sup>. As an alternative to Hi-C, the TCC protocol was also adapted for barley. Nuclei were prepared from barley leaf tissue as described above for Hi-C, before biotinylating the isolated chromatin using EZlink Iodoacetyl-PEG2-Biotin. The samples were neutralized with SDS, and DNA was digested with HindIII, dialysed, followed by immobilization to low surface coverage using streptavidin-coated magnetic beads<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Kalhor, R., Tjong, H., Jayathilaka, N., Alber, F. &amp; Chen, L. Genome architectures revealed by tethered chromosome conformation capture and population-based modeling. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 90–98 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2987">19</a></sup>. Open DNA ends were labelled with biotin-14-dCTP using Klenow enzyme, and blunt-ended, labelled DNA products were collected from the magnetic beads by reversing the formaldehyde crosslink using proteinase K<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Kalhor, R., Tjong, H., Jayathilaka, N., Alber, F. &amp; Chen, L. Genome architectures revealed by tethered chromosome conformation capture and population-based modeling. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 90–98 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2991">19</a></sup>. Biotin-14-dCTP from non-ligated DNA ends was removed by using Exonuclease III<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Kalhor, R., Tjong, H., Jayathilaka, N., Alber, F. &amp; Chen, L. Genome architectures revealed by tethered chromosome conformation capture and population-based modeling. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 90–98 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2995">19</a></sup>. Hi-C and TCC products were mechanically sheared to fragment sizes of 200–300 bp by applying ultrasound using a Covaris S220 device followed by size-fractionation using AMPure XP beads. DNA fragments in the range between 150 and 300 bp were blunt-end repaired and A-tailed before purification through biotin–streptavidin-mediated pull-down<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Belton, J. M. et al. Hi-C: a comprehensive technique to capture the conformation of genomes. Methods 58, 268–276 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d14867e2999">58</a></sup>. Illumina paired-end adapters were ligated to the Hi-C and TCC products, respectively, followed by PCR amplification, pooling of PCR products and purification with AMPure XP beads before quantification of Hi-C/TCC libraries by qPCR for Illumina HiSeq2500 PE100 sequencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Lieberman-Aiden, E. et al. Comprehensive mapping of long-range interactions reveals folding principles of the human genome. Science 326, 289–293 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3003">20</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Nanochannel-based genome mapping</h3><p>Long-range scaffolding of genome sequence assemblies was facilitated by BioNano genome maps generated by nanochannel electrophoresis of fluorescently labelled high-molecular mass DNA obtained from flow-sorted chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Stanˇková, H. et al. BioNano genome mapping of individual chromosomes supports physical mapping and sequence assembly in complex plant genomes. Plant Biotechnol. J. 14, 1523–1531 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3015">59</a></sup>. High-molecular mass DNA was prepared from 3.5 × 10<sup>6</sup> purified chromosomes (whole genome) of barley cultivar Morex essentially following published procedures<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Lysák, M. A. et al. Flow karyotyping and sorting of mitotic chromosomes of barley (Hordeum vulgare L.). Chromosome Res. 7, 431–444 (1999)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3021">60</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Šimková, H., Cˇ íhalíková, J., Vrána, J., Lysák, M. &amp; Doležel, J. Preparation of HMW DNA from plant nuclei and chromosomes isolated from root tips. Biol. Plant. 46, 369–373 (2003)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3024">61</a></sup>. The purified chromosomes were embedded in agarose miniplugs to achieve approximate concentrations of 1 million chromosomes per 40 μl volume before being treated with proteinase K as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Šimková, H., Cˇ íhalíková, J., Vrána, J., Lysák, M. &amp; Doležel, J. Preparation of HMW DNA from plant nuclei and chromosomes isolated from root tips. Biol. Plant. 46, 369–373 (2003)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3028">61</a></sup>. DNA was labelled at Nt.<i>BspQI</i> nicking sites (GCTCTTC) by incorporation of fluorescent-dUTP nucleotide analogues using <i>Taq</i> polymerase as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Stanˇková, H. et al. BioNano genome mapping of individual chromosomes supports physical mapping and sequence assembly in complex plant genomes. Plant Biotechnol. J. 14, 1523–1531 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3039">59</a></sup>. The labelled DNA was analysed on the Irys platform (BioNano Genomics) in 191 cycles in total, generating 243 Gb of data exceeding 150 kb. On the basis of the label positions on single DNA molecules, <i>de novo</i> assembly was performed by a pairwise comparison of all single molecules and graph building<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Cao, H. et al. Rapid detection of structural variation in a human genome using nanochannel-based genome mapping technology. Gigascience 3, 34 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3046">62</a></sup>. The parameter set for large genomes was used for assembly with the IrysView software. A <i>P</i> value threshold of 10<sup>−9</sup> was used during the pairwise assembly, 10<sup>−10</sup> for extension and refinement steps and 10<sup>−14</sup> for merging contigs. A whole-genome map of 4.3 Gb was obtained (Extended Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Tab1">Table 1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Data integration for constructing pseudomolecules</h3><p>The construction of pseudomolecules representing the seven barley chromosomes followed an iterative, mainly automated procedure which involved the integration of the following major datasets: (1) sequence assemblies of 87,075 unique, successfully sequenced and assembled BAC clones; (2) BAC assembly information from a genome-wide physical map of barley<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Ariyadasa, R. et al. A sequence-ready physical map of barley anchored genetically by two million single-nucleotide polymorphisms. Plant Physiol. 164, 412–423 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3072">16</a></sup>; (3) 571,814 end-sequences of BAC clones<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="International Barley Genome Sequencing Consortium. A physical, genetic and functional sequence assembly of the barley genome. Nature 491, 711–716 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3076">7</a></sup>; (4) a dense linkage map assigning genetic positions to 791,177 contigs of a whole-genome shotgun assembly of barley cultivar Morex<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Mascher, M. et al. Anchoring and ordering NGS contig assemblies by population sequencing (POPSEQ). Plant J. 76, 718–727 (2013)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3080">17</a></sup>; (5) Hi-C/TCC sequence information; and (6) the optical map of the genome of barley cultivar Morex. A schematic outline of the procedure is presented elsewhere<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Beier, S. et al. Construction of a map-based reference genome sequence for barley, Hordeum vulgare L. Sci. Data 4, 170044 (2017)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3084">22</a></sup>. In the first step, overlaps between individual BAC assemblies were searched with Megablast<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Zhang, Z., Schwartz, S., Wagner, L. &amp; Miller, W. A greedy algorithm for aligning DNA sequences. J. Comput. Biol. 7, 203–214 (2000)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3088">63</a></sup> by either applying ‘stringent’ or ‘permissive’ alignment criteria<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Beier, S. et al. Construction of a map-based reference genome sequence for barley, Hordeum vulgare L. Sci. Data 4, 170044 (2017)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3093">22</a></sup> and by combining with the high density genetic map information. On the basis of this initial analysis, a BAC overlap graph was constructed by use of the R package igraph<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Csardi, G. &amp; Nepusz, T. The igraph software package for complex network research. InterJournal Complex Syst. 1695 (2006)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3097">64</a></sup> considering the above-listed additional datasets in subsequent iterative steps. Building the overlap graph focused first on overlaps obtained under ‘stringent’ search criteria for BACs within individual physical map contigs (FP contigs) and then subsequently also between independent FP contigs. Subsequently, overlaps obtained under ‘permissive’ criteria were evaluated while checking for cumulative evidences provided by the additional datasets supporting the overlap information<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Beier, S. et al. Construction of a map-based reference genome sequence for barley, Hordeum vulgare L. Sci. Data 4, 170044 (2017)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3101">22</a></sup>. Ordering and orienting of the resultant sequence scaffolds were achieved by integrating the overlap graph with Hi-C /TCC data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Beier, S. et al. Construction of a map-based reference genome sequence for barley, Hordeum vulgare L. Sci. Data 4, 170044 (2017)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3105">22</a></sup>. Before the construction of pseudomolecules, we (1) identified genes incomplete or missing in the non-redundant sequence, but represented by (a) BAC sequence that had been excluded from the construction of the non-redundant sequence, or by (b) Morex WGS contigs, and (2) performed a final scan for contaminant sequences. Then a single FASTA file containing a single entry for each barley chromosome (a ‘pseudomolecule’) and an additional entry combining all sequences not anchored to chromosomes was constructed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Beier, S. et al. Construction of a map-based reference genome sequence for barley, Hordeum vulgare L. Sci. Data 4, 170044 (2017)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3109">22</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Three-dimensional chromatin conformation analysis</h3><p>Mapping of Hi-C/TCC reads and assignment to restriction fragments were performed as described elsewhere<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Beier, S. et al. Construction of a map-based reference genome sequence for barley, Hordeum vulgare L. Sci. Data 4, 170044 (2017)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3121">22</a></sup>. Briefly, raw reads were trimmed with cutadapt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Martin, M. Cutadapt removes adapter sequences from high-throughput sequencing reads. EMBnet. J. 17, 10–12 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3125">65</a></sup>. Trimmed Hi-C reads were mapped to the barley pseudomolecule sequence with BWA mem (version 0.7.12)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Preprint at &#xA;                    https://arxiv.org/abs/1303.3997&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3129">66</a></sup>. Duplicate removal and sorting were performed with NovoSort (<a href="http://www.novocraft.com/products/novosort/">http://www.novocraft.com/products/novosort/</a>). Mapped reads were assigned to restriction fragments with BEDtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Quinlan, A. R. &amp; Hall, I. M. BEDTools: a flexible suite of utilities for comparing genomic features. Bioinformatics 26, 841–842 (2010)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3140">67</a></sup>, tabulated with custom AWK scripts and imported into R (<a href="https://www.r-project.org/">https://www.r-project.org/</a>). Raw counts of Hi-C links were aggregated in 1 Mb bins and normalized separately for intra- and interchromosomal contacts using HiCNorm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Hu, M. et al. HiCNorm: removing biases in Hi-C data via Poisson regression. Bioinformatics 28, 3131–3133 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3152">68</a></sup>. Contact probability matrices were plotted using standard R functions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="R Core Team. R: a language and environment for statistical computing (R Foundation for Statistical Computing, 2015)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3156">69</a></sup>. Principal component analysis was performed with the R function prcomp() on the matrix of log-transformed normalized Hi-C link counts between 1 Mb fragments.</p><p>We fitted the linear model log<sub>10</sub>(nl) ~ log<sub>10</sub>(dist) + abs(cen_dist1 – cen_dist2) + arm1:arm2 + apos1:apos1 using the R function lm(). Here, nl is the normalized link count between two 1 Mb bins, dist is their distance in the linear genome, cen_dist1 and cen_dist2 are the relative distances from the centromere of both loci, arm1 and arm2 are the chromosome arm assignment of both loci, and apos1 and apos2 are the relative distances of both loci from the ends of the chromosome arm (that is, apos1 is close to zero if locus 1 is either near the centromere or the telomere, and close to one if locus 1 resides in interstitial regions). TCC reads of Morex × Barke F<sub>1</sub> hybrids were mapped to a synthetic reference representing the parental genomes. An <i>in silico</i> Barke assembly was created by inserting SNPs discovered by aligning Barke WGS reads to the Morex reference assembly with BWA MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Preprint at &#xA;                    https://arxiv.org/abs/1303.3997&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3172">66</a></sup> and calling variants with SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Li, H. A statistical framework for SNP calling, mutation discovery, association mapping and population genetical parameter estimation from sequencing data. Bioinformatics 27, 2987–2993 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3177">70</a></sup>. SNPs were then inserted into the Morex reference using the FastaAlternateReferenceMaker of GATK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="DePristo, M. A. et al. A framework for variation discovery and genotyping using next-generation DNA sequencing data. Nat. Genet. 43, 491–498 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3181">71</a></sup>. TCC reads of the hybrid were then mapped to the synthetic reference as described above. Only uniquely alignable read pairs were considered. Hi-C link counts were tabulated at the level of chromosomes.</p><p>Fluorescence <i>in situ</i> hybridization was performed with <i>H. vulgare</i> nuclei as described earlier<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Aliyeva-Schnorr, L. et al. Cytogenetic mapping with centromeric bacterial artificial chromosomes contigs shows that this recombination-poor region comprises more than half of barley chromosome 3H. Plant J. 84, 385–394 (2015)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3194">72</a></sup> using <i>Arabidopsis</i>-type telomere and barley centromere-specific [AGGGAG]<sub>5</sub> repeat probes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Hudakova, S. et al. Sequence organization of barley centromeres. Nucleic Acids Res. 29, 5029–5035 (2001)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3204">73</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Automated annotation of transcribed regions</h3><p>Automated gene annotation of the barley reference sequence assembly was based on four datasets providing independent gene evidence information (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 3</a>). This included (1) RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) data; (2) reference protein predictions from barley<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="International Barley Genome Sequencing Consortium. A physical, genetic and functional sequence assembly of the barley genome. Nature 491, 711–716 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3219">7</a></sup>, rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3223">74</a></sup>, <i>B. distachyon</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763–768 (2010)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3229">75</a></sup> and <i>S. bicolor</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3236">76</a></sup>; (3) published barley full-length complementary DNA (fl-cDNA) sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Matsumoto, T. et al. Comprehensive sequence analysis of 24,783 barley full-length cDNAs derived from 12 clone libraries. Plant Physiol. 156, 20–28 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3240">77</a></sup>; and (4) newly generated barley PacBio Iso-Seq data. Previously published<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="International Barley Genome Sequencing Consortium. A physical, genetic and functional sequence assembly of the barley genome. Nature 491, 711–716 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3244">7</a></sup> and newly generated RNA-seq datasets were derived from a total of 16 different tissues, each with three biological replicates, including seven vegetative, six inflorescence, two developing grain and one germinating grain tissues. RNA-seq libraries were sequenced on Illumina HiSeq2000 in paired-end 2 × 100 nucleotides (PE100) mode (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 3</a>). To support gene calling in general, and the identification of alternative splice forms in particular, enriched full-length transcript information was generated by the Iso-Seq method using the PacBio RS II system and DNA Sequencing Chemistry 4.0 version 2 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 3</a>). RNA-seq-based transcript structures, reference-based gene model predictions, structure information from Iso-Seq alignments as well as structure information from flcDNA sequence alignments were clustered into a consensus transcript set using Cuffcompare<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562–578 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3254">78</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 3</a>). Predicted transcript sequences were automatically extracted into a single FASTA file on the basis of respective coordinates in the genome assembly. Putative open reading frames and corresponding peptide sequences, including prediction of Pfam domains, were obtained by applying TransDecoder (<a href="https://transdecoder.github.io">https://transdecoder.github.io</a>), which also resulted in reports about predicted alternative peptides per transcript (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 3</a>). A single best translation per transcript was selected on the basis of BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403–410 (1990)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3272">79</a></sup> comparison of all predicted peptides to a comprehensive protein database containing high-confidence protein sequences from <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3278">80</a></sup>, maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3283">47</a></sup>, <i>B. distachyon</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763–768 (2010)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3289">75</a></sup>, rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763–768 (2010)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3293">75</a></sup> and <i>S. bicolor</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3299">76</a></sup>, followed by additional filtering procedures (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 3</a>). Functional descriptions (‘human readable descriptions’) were generated for all potential genes using the AHRD pipeline (<a href="https://github.com/groupschoof/AHRD">https://github.com/groupschoof/AHRD</a>) on the basis of one representative protein sequence for each gene locus. Gene candidates were then classified into high- and low-confidence genes and further subdivided into nine classes, each supported by different levels of gene evidence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 3</a>). High-confidence protein-coding genes either showed significant sequence homology to a reference protein or were associated with a predicted function. Low-confidence genes were characterized by (1) having no or only weak sequence homology to reference proteins and no predicted function, (2) they were candidates for transposons or (3) they lacked an open reading frame of a minimal length (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 3</a>). Completeness of gene-space representation was evaluated with the BUSCO pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3320">23</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig7">Extended Data Fig. 2b</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Feature distributions along the chromosomes</h3><p>A sliding window approach with a window size of 4 Mb and a shift of 0.8 Mb was used to display the distribution of different genome components and other features such as GC content or recombination rate along the chromosomes. The resulting data were smoothed with the python function scipy.signal.gaussian (p1 = 40, p2 = 10 for <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>; p1 = 15, p2 = 3 for <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>). The boundaries of genomic compartments (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>) are given in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Table 4.4</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Annotation of the non-genic part of the genome</h3><p>Transposable elements were detected and classified by homology search with Vmatch (<a href="http://www.vmatch.de">http://www.vmatch.de</a>) against the REdat_9.7_Triticeae section of the PGSB transposon library<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Spannagl, M. et al. PGSB PlantsDB: updates to the database framework for comparative plant genome research. Nucleic Acids Res. 44 (D1), D1141–D1147 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3362">81</a></sup>. The following parameter settings were used: identity ≥70%, minimal hit length 75 bp, seed length 12 bp (exact commandline: -d -p -l 75 -identity 70 -seedlength 12 -exdrop 5). The Vmatch output was filtered for redundant hits by prioritizing higher-scoring matches and then either shortening (&lt;90% coverage and ≥50 bp rest length) or removing lower-scoring overlaps.</p><p>The identification of full-length LTR retrotransposons with LTRharvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 18 (2008)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3369">82</a></sup> resulted in 143,957 non-overlapping candidate sequences using the following parameter settings: ‘overlaps best -seed 30 -minlenltr 100 -maxlenltr 2000 -mindistltr 3000 -maxdistltr 25000 -similar 85 -mintsd 4 -maxtsd 20 -motif tgca -motifmis 1 -vic 60 -xdrop 5 -mat 2 -mis -2 -ins -3 -del -3’. All candidates were annotated for PfamA domains with hmmer3 software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Eddy, S. R. Accelerated profile HMM searches. PLOS Comput. Biol. 7, e1002195 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3373">83</a></sup> and stringently filtered for false positives by several criteria, the main ones being the presence of at least one typical retrotransposon domain (for example, RT, RH, INT, GAG) and a tandem repeat content below 25%. This resulted in a final set of 24,952 high-confidence full-length LTR retrotransposons. Insertion ages of the LTR retrotransposons were calculated according to the method of ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="SanMiguel, P., Gaut, B. S., Tikhonov, A., Nakajima, Y. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. The paleontology of intergene retrotransposons of maize. Nat. Genet. 20, 43–45 (1998)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3376">84</a> by the divergence of 5′ and 3′ LTRs that had been identical at the time of transposition. We used a grass-specific mutation rate of 1 × 10<sup>−8</sup>. The average age of all full-length LTR elements was calculated in 4 Mb windows and plotted in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>. The frequencies of 20-mers were determined using Tallymer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Gremme, G., Steinbiss, S. &amp; Kurtz, S. GenomeTools: a comprehensive software library for efficient processing of structured genome annotations. IEEE/ACM Trans. Comput. Biol. Bioinformatics 10, 645–656 (2013)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3386">85</a></sup>.</p><p>Phylogenetic analysis of Gypsy elements was performed on predicted protein sequences deposited at the TREP database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wicker, T., Matthews, D. E. &amp; Keller, B. TREP: a database for Triticeae repetitive elements. Trends Plant Sci. 7, 561–562 (2002)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3393">32</a></sup>. Protein domains in predicted open reading frames were identified with Pfam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Bateman, A. et al. The Pfam protein families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, D138–D141 (2004)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3397">86</a></sup>, SignalP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Petersen, T. N., Brunak, S., von Heijne, G. &amp; Nielsen, H. SignalP 4.0: discriminating signal peptides from transmembrane regions. Nat. Methods 8, 785–786 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3401">87</a></sup> and COILS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Lupas, A., Van Dyke, M. &amp; Stock, J. Predicting coiled coils from protein sequences. Science 252, 1162–1164 (1991)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3405">88</a></sup>.</p><p>For the analysis of transposable element content in up- and downstream regions of genes, 10 kb immediately flanking the predicted coding sequences of all high-confidence genes were extracted from the genome assembly. The genomic segments were then used in BLASTN searches<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403–410 (1990)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3412">79</a></sup> against the TREP database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wicker, T., Matthews, D. E. &amp; Keller, B. TREP: a database for Triticeae repetitive elements. Trends Plant Sci. 7, 561–562 (2002)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3416">32</a></sup>. After an initial annotation, previously unclassified or poorly characterized transposable element families were re-analysed and new consensus sequences were constructed. Analysis of up- and downstream regions was then repeated with the updated TREP database. The transposable element family producing the longest BLASTN hit was determined for every 20th base position of each 10 kb segment, resulting in 500 data points for each up- and downstream region of the high-confidence genes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Gene family analysis</h3><p>Gene family clusters were defined from 39,734 barley high-confidence class genes and the annotated gene sets of Rice MSU7.0 (39,049 genes, <a href="http://rice.plantbiology.msu.edu/">http://rice.plantbiology.msu.edu/</a>), <i>B. distachyon</i> version 3.1 (31,694 genes, <a href="https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/pz/portal.html%23!info?alias=Org_Bdistachyon">https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/pz/portal.html#!info?alias=Org_Bdistachyon</a>), <i>S. bicolor</i> version 3.1 (33,032 genes, <a href="https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/pz/portal.html%23!info?alias=Org_Sbicolor">https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/pz/portal.html#!info?alias=Org_Sbicolor</a>) and <i>A. thaliana</i> TAIR10 (27,416 genes, <a href="https://www.arabidopsis.org/">https://www.arabidopsis.org/</a>)) using OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3466">89</a></sup> software version 2.0. Splice variants were removed from the datasets, keeping only the representative/longest protein sequence prediction, and datasets were filtered for internal stop codons and incompatible reading frames. In the first step, pairwise sequence similarities between all input protein sequences were calculated using BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403–410 (1990)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3470">79</a></sup> with an <i>e</i>-value cut-off of 10<sup>−5</sup>. Markov clustering of the resulting similarity matrix was used to define the orthologue cluster structure, using an inflation value (-I) of 1.5 (OrthoMCL default). Gene families with barley-specific gene duplications, compared with other plant species, were extracted from the ENSEMBL Compara pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Bolser, D., Staines, D. M., Pritchard, E. &amp; Kersey, P. Ensembl Plants: integrating tools for visualizing, mining, and analyzing plant genomics data. Methods Mol. Biol. 1374, 115–140 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3480">90</a></sup>. Over- and under-representation of Gene Ontology terms between barley and other plant species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Tables 4.1–4.3</a>) and between genomic compartments (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Table 4.5</a>) were analysed with a hypergeometric test using the functions GOstats and GSEABase from the Bioconductor R package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Gentleman, R. C. et al. Bioconductor: open software development for computational biology and bioinformatics. Genome Biol. 5, R80 (2004)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3490">91</a></sup> against a universe of all genes with Gene Ontology annotations. REViGO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Supek, F., Bošnjak, M., Škunca, N. &amp; Šmuc, T. REVIGO summarizes and visualizes long lists of Gene Ontology terms. PLoS ONE 6, e21800 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3494">92</a></sup>, which removes redundant and similar terms from long Gene Ontology lists by semantic clustering, was applied to visualize the enrichment results. Expansion of three barley gene families encoding α-amylases, the vacuolar processing enzyme VPE2 protein subfamily and the sugar transporters SWEET11 subfamily, with specific importance in barley grain filling/seed development or barley germination/malting, were analysed in greater detail using BLAST searches (versus genome and gene prediction) as well as GenomeThreader mappings to the barley genome assembly. Further details are provided in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 4</a>. <i>In situ</i> hybridizations for SWEET genes were performed as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Radchuk, V., Weier, D., Radchuk, R., Weschke, W. &amp; Weber, H. Development of maternal seed tissue in barley is mediated by regulated cell expansion and cell disintegration and coordinated with endosperm growth. J. Exp. Bot. 62, 1217–1227 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3505">93</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Analysis of sequence and haplotype diversity</h3><p>Ninety-six two-row spring (<i>n</i> = 48) and winter (<i>n</i> = 48) homozygous inbred elite barley lines (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Table 5.1</a>) were subjected to exome capture using the barley Roche NimbleGen exome capture liquid array<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Mascher, M. et al. Barley whole exome capture: a tool for genomic research in the genus Hordeum and beyond. Plant J. 76, 494–505 (2013)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3527">94</a></sup> and sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform. An average of 2 × 21,876,780 paired-end Illumina reads per sample was generated. This corresponds to approximately 72× coverage of the 61 Mb exome capture space.</p><p>The raw Illumina reads were mapped to the reference sequence with BWA-MEM version 0.7.10 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Preprint at &#xA;                    https://arxiv.org/abs/1303.3997&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3533">66</a>), using a stringent mismatch setting of ≤2% mismatches per read. Variant calling was performed with the Genome Analysis Tool Kit (GATK)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="DePristo, M. A. et al. A framework for variation discovery and genotyping using next-generation DNA sequencing data. Nat. Genet. 43, 491–498 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3537">71</a></sup> version 3.4.0, following the GATK Best Practices pipeline (<a href="https://www.broadinstitute.org/gatk/guide/best-practices.php">https://www.broadinstitute.org/gatk/guide/best-practices.php</a>). This included read de-duplication, indel realignment, base quality score recalibration and variant calling with the latest version of the HaplotypeCaller. The workflow was implemented in a BASH script. The Tablet assembly viewer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Milne, I. et al. Tablet—next generation sequence assembly visualization. Bioinformatics 26, 401–402 (2010)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3548">95</a></sup> was used for visual spot checks of mappings and SNPs calls.</p><p>Variant discovery resulted in 15,982,580 variants in total, of which 943,959 were multi-nucleotide polymorphisms or short insertions/deletions (indels), while the remainder represented SNPs. For subsequent genetic analysis, we first reduced the total variant dataset by applying rigorous filtering criteria to produce a highly robust subset of 72,563 SNPs distributed across all seven barley chromosomes. The filtering applied was as follows: (1) ≥8× coverage for ≥50% of the samples; (2) ≥95% of samples represented at each SNP locus; (3) ≥5% minor allele frequency at the level of the sample: that is, counting sample genotypes rather than individual reads; (4) a VCF SNP quality score ≥30; and (5) ≥98% of samples homozygous. These filters reduced false-positive variant calls by removing spurious variant calls resulting from systematic read mis-mapping. Of this filtered dataset, a subset of 3,500 randomly sampled markers from each chromosome was analysed with the Haploview software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Barrett, J. C., Fry, B., Maller, J. &amp; Daly, M. J. Haploview: analysis and visualization of LD and haplotype maps. Bioinformatics 21, 263–265 (2005)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3555">96</a></sup>. This subsampling was required as Haploview was unable to generate the required plots when larger data volumes were used as input. Haploview was run on defaults, using the GABRIEL blocks method. The genotype calls were also imported into the genotype visualization software Flapjack<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Milne, I. et al. Flapjack—graphical genotype visualization. Bioinformatics 26, 3133–3134 (2010)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3559">97</a></sup> to produce chromosome-scale images of haplotype diversity within the spring and winter pools. Diversity statistics were calculated in GenAlEx version 6.502 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Peakall, R. &amp; Smouse, P. E. GenAlEx 6.5: genetic analysis in Excel. Population genetic software for teaching and research—an update. Bioinformatics 28, 2537–2539 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3562">98</a>) and rolling averages based on 100 adjacent SNPs were plotted in Microsoft Excel 2010.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Data availability</h3><p>The genome assembly for barley has been deposited in the Plant Genomics and Phenomics Research Data Repository under digital object identifier <a href="https://doi.org/10.5447/IPK/2016/34">http://dx.doi.org/10.5447/IPK/2016/34</a>. Accession numbers for all deposited datasets are listed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#MOESM40">Supplementary Note 1</a>. The barley genome assembly has been deposited on the IPK Barley Blast Server (<a href="http://webblast.ipk-gatersleben.de/barley_ibsc/">http://webblast.ipk-gatersleben.de/barley_ibsc/</a>). All other data are available from the corresponding authors upon reasonable request.</p></div></div></section>

Open reading frames were then predicted for 83,105 gene candidates. <b>b</b>, Gene candidates were classified into high-confidence (HC) and low-confidence (LC) genes on the basis of homology to reference proteins and alignment to library of repeat elements. Additional filtering procedures were applied before defining the final gene sets. Arrows between boxes with counts of high-confidence and low-confidence genes in each step indicate re-classifications (high-confidence to low-confidence, or low-confidence to high-confidence).</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig7"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature22043/figures/7" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22043/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature22043_Fig7_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 2 Assembly validation.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Conserved gene order between barley (<i>y</i> axis) and <i>B. distachyon</i> (<i>x</i> axis). <b>b</b>, Completeness of the gene annotation as assessed by BUSCO. <b>c</b>, Representation of repetitive <i>k</i>-mers in reads and assemblies. <b>d</b>, Representation of full-length LTR retrotransposons in sequence assemblies of plant genomes with different sizes (represented by black points). The map-based reference sequence of barley reported in the present paper is shown in blue. Red dots correspond to shotgun assemblies of the barley genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="International Barley Genome Sequencing Consortium. A physical, genetic and functional sequence assembly of the barley genome. Nature 491, 711–716 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3707">7</a></sup> and wheat chromosome 3B<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="The International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. A chromosome-based draft sequence of the hexaploid bread wheat (Triticum aestivum) genome. Science 345, 1251788 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d14867e3711">99</a></sup>.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig8"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature22043/figures/8" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22043/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature22043_Fig8_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 3 Hi-C contact matrices.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Intrachromosomal contacts. <b>b</b>, Interchromosomal contacts. Darker red indicates a higher contact probability.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig9"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature22043/figures/9" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22043/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature22043_Fig9_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 4 Global patterns in Hi-C contact matrices.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Principal component analysis of intrachromosomal Hi-C contact matrices. The eigenvectors of the first three principal components are plotted. Centromere positions are marked with a red line. <b>b</b>, Proportion of variance explained by linear models incorporating position informational in the linear genome fitted to the Hi-C contact matrices. <b>c</b>, Hi-C link counts in Morex × Barke F<sub>1</sub> hybrids within the same chromosome, between homologous chromosomes and between non-homologous chromosomes.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig10"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature22043/figures/10" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22043/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature22043_Fig10_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 5 Distributions of genomic features and the context of repetitive elements.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, <b>b</b>, Panels <b>a</b> and <b>b</b> are analogous to <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig1">Figs 1a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig2">2a</a>. Grey vertical connector bars and dashed lines inside sub-panels between sub-panels for each chromosome indicate centromere positions.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig11"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature22043/figures/11" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22043/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature22043_Fig11_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 6 Experimental strategy to distinguish individual <i>amy1_1</i> copies by PCR from genomic DNA through polymorphisms in the extended promoter regions of <i>amy1_1</i> full-length copies.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Experimental strategy, primers CD52_amy1fw and CD53_amy1rc bind in the extended promotor region of all full-length <i>amy1_1</i> copies (expected amplicon sizes are 225 bp for <i>amy1_1a</i>, 299 bp for <i>amy1_1b</i> and <i>amy1_1d</i> and 336 bp for <i>amy1_1c</i>). Forward primers CD54_fw1a, CD55_fw1b and CD56_fw1c are designed to specifically amplify copies <i>amy1_1a</i>, <i>amy1_1b</i> and <i>amy1_1c</i>, respectively when used with reverse primer CD58_amy1rc, which binds in the coding region of all <i>amy1_1</i> copies. Expected amplicon sizes are 1,024 bp (<i>amy1_1a</i>), 1,026 bp (<i>amy1_1b</i>) and 757 bp (<i>amy1_1c</i>). Primer pair (CD55_fw1b–CD58_amy1rc) further binds to copy <i>amy1_1d</i>: here, sequences of the expected amplicons contain sufficient polymorphisms to distinguish these copies from each other. Positions of selected sequence polymorphisms and deleted regions suitable to distinguish single copies are indicated as black vertical bars and gaps, respectively. Numbering was done in respect of copy <i>amy1_1b</i>. <b>b</b>, PCR amplification of <i>amy1_1</i> promoter regions in six barley cultivars and landraces. As expected, a PCR for cultivar Morex, using universal primers CD52_amy1fw and CD53_amy1rc, resulted in three amplicons of the expected sizes 225, 299 and 336 bp (compare <b>a</b>), which was confirmed by Sanger sequencing. Further primers CD52_amy1fw and CD53_amy1rc were used to amplify the <i>amy1_1</i> extended promoter region in various barley cultivars. These experiments indicate polymorphic variation in, or even absence of, single promoters of <i>amy1_1</i> in the different cultivars. The cultivars analysed differ in row type (six-rowed: cultivars Morex, Masan Naked 1, Akashinriki, Etincel; two-rowed: cultivars Barke, Bowman), growth habit (spring barley: cultivars Morex, Barke, Bowman, Masan Naked 1, Akashinriki; winter barley: cultivar Etincel) and geographic origin (North America: cultivars Morex, Bowman; Europe: cultivars Barke, Etincel; Asia: cultivars Masan Naked 1, Akashinriki). The cultivars Masan Naked 1 and Akashinriki depict landraces used for food, Bowman was classified as non-malting barley, while Morex, Barke and Etincel represent modern malting barley. <b>c</b>, Copy-specific PCR amplification of <i>amy1_1</i> extended promoter regions. PCR amplification and Sanger sequencing identified three <i>amy1_1</i> copies in barley cultivar Morex: <i>amy1_1a</i> (CD54_fw1a–CD58_amy1rc), <i>amy1_1b</i> (CD55_fw1b–CD58_amy1rc) and <i>amy1_1c</i> (CD56_fw1c–CD58_amy1rc). Additionally, sequencing revealed two polymorphic sites in PCR amplicon <i>amy1_1b</i> (CD55_fw1b–CD58_amy1rc) at positions 721 bp (T/C) and 1175 bp (C/T) (see <b>a</b>), indicating the presence of one or two additional <i>amy1_1b</i>-like copies in the genome of the analysed individual. The presence of copy <i>amy1-1d</i> could not be confirmed. The reason for that might have been sequence deviations in the cultivar Morex accession used for BAC library construction versus that used for the presented experiments, or differences in PCR efficiency for amplification of copies <i>amy1_1b</i> and <i>amy1_1d</i>.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig12"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature22043/figures/12" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22043/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature22043_Fig12_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 7 SWEET gene expression.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Control experiment for mRNA <i>in situ</i> hybridizations shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22043#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>. <i>In situ</i> hybridization with sense probes for <i>SWEET11a</i> (top) and <i>SWEET11b</i> (bottom). Scale bars, 100 μm. <b>b</b>, Expression of <i>SWEET11a</i> and <i>SWEET11b</i>. Results of qPCR in different plant organs and in the developing grains at 7 days after flowering (DAF).</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig13"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature22043/figures/13" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22043/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature22043_Fig13_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 8 Haplotype blocks in sets of 48 samples each of elite two-row spring barley lines (top half of each chromosome’s figure) and winter barley lines (bottom half), separately for each chromosome.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>We restricted the number of SNPs per chromosome by randomly choosing 3,500 to fit with the maximum permitted by the software. The red and green plots in the centre of each chromosome figure represent whole-canvas dumps produced with the Flapjack software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Milne, I. et al. Flapjack—graphical genotype visualization. Bioinformatics 26, 3133–3134 (2010)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d14867e4008">97</a></sup>. Markers are arranged in columns in linear order along the chromosome; red pixels represent reference alleles, while green pixels represent alternative alleles. Each row represents a barley cultivar; these have been sorted top to bottom by year of introduction (ascending). The Flapjack plots are framed by cropped linkage disequilibrium plots generated with the HaploView software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Barrett, J. C., Fry, B., Maller, J. &amp; Daly, M. J. Haploview: analysis and visualization of LD and haplotype maps. Bioinformatics 21, 263–265 (2005)" href="/articles/nature22043#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d14867e4012">96</a></sup>. Colour intensity conveys the extent of linkage between pairs of markers (red, highest). Approximate centromere positions are indicated by semi-opaque grey squares. The triangles with the thin black outline represent haplotype blocks as computed by HaploView. In some regions, extensive stretches exist where no blocks were detected (for example, chr2H, spring lines in top half, near centromere). These generally present highly monomorphic regions where there is no evidence for multiple haplotypes, and consequently blocks were not called.</p></div></div><div             <aside><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__container" data-component-show-more="true" id="editorial-summary"><section><h1 class="c-article-editorial-summary__title u-h3">Editorial Summary</h1><h2 class="c-article-editorial-summary__article-title">Barley genome sequenced</h2><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__content c-article-editorial-summary__content--less"><p>Triticeae grasses, which include barley, wheat and rye, are widely cultivated plants with particularly complex genomes and evolutionary histories. Sequencing of the barley genome has been particularly challenging owing to its large size and particular genomic features, such as an abundance of repetitive elements. Nils Stein and colleagues of the International Barley Genome Sequencing Consortium report sequencing and assembly of a reference genome for barley (<i>Hordeumvulgare</i> L). They use a combined approach of hierarchical shotgun sequencing of bacterial artificial chromosomes, genome mapping on nanochannel arrays and chromosome-scale scaffolding with Hi-C sequencing. This brings the first comprehensive, completely ordered assembly of the pericentromeric regions of a Triticeae genome. The authors also sequenced and examined genetic diversity in the exomes of 96 European elite barley lines with a spring or winter growth habit, and highlight the utility of this resource for cereal genomics and breeding programs.</p></div></section></div></aside>
